It occurs on the tongue mistakenly contrary to reality, rather its indication is only upon intent. (It was said) to rebut this view: (Necessarily trust is lost in reporting, for then it would be possible that his statement from Allah the Exalted be a lie occurring on his noble tongue mistakenly, since the listener has no evidence of the inner state except reports. If lying is allowed in it, even mistakenly, trust ceases.) (And it was answered: The necessary consequence at the time of the occurrence of lying mistakenly on the tongue is (the notification) that it is contrary to reality. So when there is no notification, that is evidence of truthfulness, so trust remains. And the objector could return and say: It necessarily means that the ummah has no trust in the truthfulness of his reports at the time of hearing, rather they wait until the time of notification, and adherence to that is far from etiquette and fairness, so reflect. As for other than lying from major sins and base minor sins) like stealing a morsel and other things that indicate baseness, even if permissible, (there is consensus) among the sects of Islam on their infallibility from committing them intentionally, (by hearing) from the Ahl al-Sunnah, the suppressors of innovation, may Allah increase them, (or by reason) from the Mu'tazila and Rawafid, may Allah forsake them. You have known their suspicion and its answer. (And) also consensus on allowing them unintentionally and mistakenly, whether it be an error in ijtihad or intending the permissible in the forbidden. And the secret in allowing that is that it is not truly a sin, and from it is the taslim at the end of two rak'ahs forgetfully. (Except the Shia) for they do not allow them even forgetfully, but they allow them intentionally as dissimulation. It has passed. (And it is permissible to deliberately commit) minor sins other than them, i.e., other than major sins and base minor sins, (without) persistence, for persistence in a minor sin is a major sin according to most Shafi'is and Mu'tazila with cold suspicions, as in His statement, the Exalted: 'And she desired him and he would have desired her if he had not seen the proof of his Lord' [Yusuf: 24] and similar examples from the story of Dawud and Sulayman, peace be upon them. And the answer: that from Yusuf, peace be upon him, no desire for zinā occurred at all; the meaning is: Zulaykha desired to kill Yusuf because of his refusal to do what she commanded, and Yusuf desired to kill her because she was intent on causing him to fall into the unlawful. And if he had not seen the proof of his Lord, he would have killed her. Or the meaning: she desired zinā, and Yusuf, peace be upon him, did not desire. And if he had not seen the proof of his Lord, he would have desired. And it is not intended that desire occurred from him, and had he not seen the proof of the Lord he would have done it. How, while there is no trace of it in the speech, and it is not befitting the station of the Exalted that He should praise Yusuf for this desire. These verses are brought for praise, as appears with minimal reflection. As for the story of Dawud, it is not authentic, for it was not transmitted from the Messenger of Allah, may Allah bless him and his family and companions, by a sound chain; rather historians took it from Jewish books, so no heed is paid to it. What can be relied upon is that Uriah was a suitor to a woman, and Dawud, peace be upon him, married her. The most of this is leaving the better choice, and there is no sin in it, neither minor nor major. As for the story of Sulayman, the sound and relied-upon account is that he said: 'Tonight I will approach my wives, and they will all give birth to a horseman who will fight in the way of Allah the Exalted,' and he did not say 'if Allah wills,' as narrated in Sahih al-Bukhari. Perhaps coupling the exception with the speech was not obligatory at that time, so there is nothing in it except leaving the better choice. Everything else that historians transmit is all a mistake that should not be paid attention to, except by one who dares to stray from the noose of Islam. So be certain and do not be confused. (And the Hanafis, may Allah be pleased with them, prevented) i.e., the occurrence of base minor sins. (I say: That is the truth, for their minor sin is major in their right, even if it is minor in our right. Do you not see that the permissible of the common people are the sins of the pious? Do you not see what Dawud al-Ta'i, the imam, the gnostic, said: 'Holding back 200 dirhams for a year is forbidden for the Sufi who claims love of Allah the Exalted,' and it is obligatory to pay zakat on it: 5 dirhams and 200 dirhams for us? And for the common people, it is permissible and one-quarter of a tenth is obligatory. (And the good deeds of the pious are the sins of the near ones.) Do you not see what al-Sari al-Saqati, that imam, said: 'I seek forgiveness from Allah for my saying Alhamdulillah when a man told me of the great fire in the market and the safety of my shop.' Since the prophets, peace be upon them, are the heads of the near ones, our minor sin is major in their right, so their occurrence from them is not sound. So understand, for it is the truth, do not be confused, and be certain. This completes the discussion regarding what is after prophethood. As for before prophethood, the investigation is, and the people of Allah from the noble Sufis affirm, that they are also infallible from major and minor sins intentionally. How not, for they are born on walayah and not a blink of an eye passes while they are not beholding Allah the Exalted. Their walayah is strong, taken from the walayah of the awliya who are not suspect, and the awliya are protected from sins. So understand and be certain. (And) both the Hanafis and Shafi'is (allowed the zalla in them), i.e., major and minor sins after prophethood and before it, by intending the permissible which results in (a sin), i.e., it results in something that would be a sin if done intentionally, (like the punch of Musa, peace and blessings be upon him and our Prophet, to the Copt, so he died and the killing was necessitated. That was when he took an Israelite to carry wood on him to the kitchen of Pharaoh, and he refused. So they argued, and the Israelite sought help from Musa. So Musa forbade the Copt from what he was doing, but he did not stop. Rather it is said: He said, 'I intended to carry you,' so Musa, peace and blessings be upon him and our Prophet, punched him as discipline, and he passed away and died. So this is a zalla from him before prophethood. And he chose the example from before prophethood only to indicate that the state before prophethood and after it are equal in not committing sin, even minor, except in the manner of zalla. And the benefit of the occurrence of zalla from them, peace and prayer be upon them, is to test them so that they seek forgiveness and repent and attain lofty rank. (And the zalla is accompanied) by a notification from the doer or from Allah the Exalted by revelation, so that imitation in it does not occur and the test is achieved. Then the zalla does not involve disobedience in one sense, rather it is permissible, as Allah the Exalted said: 'It is not for a believer to kill a believer except by mistake' [al-Nisa: 92]. And know that just as zalla is allowed for them, peace be upon them, so is error; they do something that would be a sin if it were not an error, and likewise forgetfulness. And the criticism of some against the greatest shaykh, the seal of Muhammadian walayah, may his secret be sanctified, for his ruling that error occurred from Ibrahim, peace be upon him and our Prophet and his family and companions, in interpreting his dream that he was about to sacrifice his son, only arose from the dominance of desire over him, so it does not deserve attention.
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a detailed discussion of prophetic infallibility (ʻisma) within a usul al-fiqh context. The author, commenting on the matn of Muslim al-thubut, addresses the crucial question: can prophets commit sins, especially minor ones? The passage clarifies that while there is consensus on major sins, minor sins are debated. The Hanafi position is that prophets are immune from all sins intentionally, even minor ones, because for them a 'minor' sin is like a major sin due to their high rank. However, the possibility of a 'zalla' (a slip that results in something that would be a sin if intentional) is accepted, as illustrated by the story of Musa (AS) killing the Copt accidentally. The author refutes objections about trust in prophetic reports by explaining that the very absence of subsequent correction (tanbih) confirms truthfulness. He also analyzes Quranic verses and stories of Yusuf, Dawud, and Sulayman (AS) to show that no sin occurred—only 'tark al-awla' (leaving the better option). The passage ends by noting that even before prophethood, prophets are protected, and both Hanafis and Shafi'is allow zalla. The key takeaway is that prophetic sinlessness is maintained categorically, with subtle allowance for unintentional slips that are not true sins.

Key Terms
زلة (zalla): A slip or unintended act that results in something that would be a sin if done intentionally.
تنبيه (tanbīh): Notification; divine or prophetic correction of an error.
سهو (sahw): Forgetfulness; unintentional omission or addition in worship.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the purpose of zalla (slip) occurring to prophets, according to the passage?
A) To demonstrate that prophets are fallible like humans.
B) To test them so they seek forgiveness and attain higher rank.
C) To show that divine protection can fail.
D) To allow them to commit minor sins without blame.

Key Takeaway
Zalla is an unintentional slip, not a sin, allowed for prophets as a test that leads to repentance and elevation, always accompanied by notification to prevent imitation.

Definition of Khabar in Usul
Debates whether the term 'khabar' can be defined

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi usul scholars hold that 'khabar' cannot be defined by a real definition and distinguish between real and verbal definitions.

Core Idea
The term 'khabar' is self-evident (badihi); its definition is verbal only, not a real definition revealing essence.

In this lesson, we explore a foundational usul debate: can the term 'khabar' (report) be defined? The passage presents the view that 'khabar' is a necessary concept—everyone knows intuitively what a report is. Thus, any attempt to define it is merely verbal, not a real definition. Understanding this distinction is critical for grasping the Hanafi approach to textual evidence.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of debate
Read the passage and locate the phrase 'اختلف في تحديده'. What exactly is being debated? Is it whether 'khabar' can be defined at all, or what kind of definition is possible?
The debate is about the possibility of a real definition (haqiqi) versus a verbal one.
2
Understand Al-Ghazali's position
What does Al-Ghazali say? He states that 'khabar' cannot be defined 'بسبب العسرة' (because of difficulty) and is known by division and example. What does this imply about the nature of the concept?
Al-Ghazali holds that 'khabar' is not amenable to a standard definition but can be identified through examples.
3
Grasp the majority's view
The passage says 'والأكثر قالوا لا يحد لأن علمه ضروري'. Why does the majority argue that 'khabar' cannot be defined? What does 'daruri' mean in this context?
Daruri means self-evident or necessary knowledge—something known without need for definition.
4
Distinguish real vs. verbal definitions
The text concludes that definitions are all verbal (لفظيات). Explain the difference between a real definition (which captures the essence) and a verbal definition (which merely clarifies usage). Which type applies to 'khabar'?
A real definition is impossible for self-evident concepts; only verbal definitions that help explain the term are possible.
5
Identify the chosen view
The author says 'وهو المختار'. Which view does the author prefer? Why is this the operative position in Hanafi usul?
The chosen view is that of the majority: 'khabar' cannot be defined because its conception is necessary.

Source Text

ثم اعلم أن حجية السنة موقوفة بالنسبة إلينا على السند وإن لم تكن موقوفة عليه بالنسبة إلى الصحابة (وهو الإخبار عن طريق المتن بأن يقول : حدثني فلان من غير واسطة أو بها أنه قال رسول الله ، وهذا خبر خاص، فلا بد من البحث عن الخبر (والخبر قد اختلف في تحديده) هل يصح أو لا؟ فقيل لا يحد) ثم اختلفوا (فالغزالي) الإمام حجة الإسلام قدس سره قال : لا يحد العسره، وإنما يعرف بالقسمة والمثال) بالوجه (كما قال في الوجود والعلم) ومقصوده أنه لا يمكن معرفته بالكنه ولا بالرسم إلا بوجه ما انقسم إلى هذه الأقسام أو ما يماثل هذا الجزئي ونحوهما والأكثر) قالوا لا يحد لأن علمه أي تصوره (ضروري) وهو لا يحد تعريفاً حقيقياً، والمعرفات كلها لفظيات (وهو المختار) عند المصنف وغيره فإن كل أحد يعلم بالضرورة أنه (موجود) وهو خبر خاص ومطلق الخبر تمام ماهية هذا الخبر) فيلزم من بداهته
Then know that the authority of the Sunnah, with respect to us, depends on the chain (sanad), although it did not depend on it with respect to the Companions. (It is: reporting via the chain about the text, saying: 'So-and-so narrated to me, without intermediary or with it, that he said: The Messenger of Allah said...' And this is a specific report. So it is necessary to investigate the report. (And the report has differed concerning its definition): Is it valid or not? For it is said: It cannot be defined. Then they differed. (Al-Ghazali) the Imam, the Proof of Islam, may his secret be sanctified, said: It cannot be defined because of difficulty; rather it is known by division and example, by way, as he said regarding existence and knowledge. His intention is that it cannot be known by its essence nor by a description except by a certain way that it is divided into these divisions or similar to this particular, and the like. (And the majority) said it cannot be defined because knowledge of it, i.e., its conception, is (necessary), and the necessary cannot be defined by a real definition, and all definitions are verbal. (And it is the chosen view) of the author and others, for every person knows necessarily that it (exists) and it is a specific report, and the absolute report is the complete essence of this report, so it follows from its self-evidence.
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage transitions from the discussion of prophetic infallibility to the fundamental usul topic of the definition of 'khabar' (report/hadith). The author first reminds that for us, the authority of the Sunnah depends on the chain of transmission (sanad), whereas the Companions had direct access. Then he defines khabar in a specific sense: it is a report about the text of a hadith through a chain. The core issue addressed is whether 'khabar' as a term can be defined. The text presents a debate: some say it cannot be defined. Al-Ghazali holds that it is known by division and example, not by a real definition. The majority, and the author's chosen view, affirm that it cannot be defined by a true definition because its conception is necessary (badihi) — everyone knows what a report is intuitively. Therefore, any 'definition' is merely verbal, not a real definition that reveals the essence. This is a key methodological point in usul al-fiqh: concepts that are self-evident are not subject to formal definition.

Key Terms
خبر (khabar): A report about the prophetic statement via a chain; a specific report (khabar khas) contrasted with absolute report (mutlaq al-khabar).
تعريف (taʻrīf): Definition; the attempt to define khabar is problematic because its conception is necessary (badihi).
ضروري (ḍarūrī): Necessary knowledge; self-evident conceptualization that does not require definition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the chosen view (al-mukhtar) of the author regarding the definition of 'khabar'?
A) It should be defined by its genus and differentia.
B) It cannot be defined because its conception is necessary (daruri).
C) It can be defined by example only.
D) It must be defined by the chain of transmission.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi usul position is that 'khabar' is a self-evident concept; it cannot be given a real definition, only a verbal one.

Definition and Nature of Khabar — a Usul Dispute
Debates the definition of khabar and its epistemological status

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school holds that the concept of khabar (report) is self-evident and evaluate the objections based on the presential/acquired knowledge distinction.

Core Idea
Khabar cannot be defined — its conception is necessary (daruri), known by every rational person by self-evidence.

The authority of the Sunnah hangs on the concept of a 'report' (khabar). But what is a report? Can it be defined, or is its meaning so basic that everyone knows it by necessity? This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut wades into a deep usul dispute: some scholars attempt a real definition, while the Hanafi majority insists that the conception of khabar is self-evident (daruri). The discussion weaves through al-Ghazali's intermediate view, the distinction between presential and acquired knowledge, and the nature of narration (hikaya). By the end, you will grasp why the Hanafi school relies on self-evidence and how this shapes the foundation of hadith authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested issue
Determine whether khabar can be defined by a real definition (hadd haqiqi) or is known directly without definition.
The passage opens by noting ikhtilaf on this issue.
2
State the three positions
(a) al-Ghazali: khabar is known by division and example, not by a single term. (b) The majority (Hanafis): conception is self-evident (daruri); definitions are only verbal. (c) A few others: it can be defined.
The author chooses the majority view as 'al-mukhtar'.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
The majority argues that every person knows by necessity what a report is. Attempts at a real definition are flawed because the concept is already present in the mind.
The text uses the example of a specific report (khabar khas) to anchor self-evidence.
4
Examine the objection and response
An objection: self-evidence of the specific report does not guarantee self-evidence of the absolute report, due to the difference between presential (huduri) and acquired (husuli) knowledge. The Hanafi response insists that the specific report is conceived in its essence, so the absolute follows.
This part involves the technical distinction between ilm huduri and ilm husuli.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The text reaffirms the majority view, noting that the specific report is self-evident in essence, and the absolute is self-evident because its essential properties are necessarily implied.
Despite counterarguments, the chosen position is that khabar cannot be defined by a real definition.

It is reporting about the way of the text, by saying: 'So-and-so narrated to me, without or with an intermediary, that he said: The Messenger of Allah said...' Then they differed: Al-Ghazali, the Imam, Hujjat al-Islam, may his secret be sanctified, said: It cannot be defined in one word; rather it is known by division and example, in a certain respect, as he said regarding existence and knowledge. His intent is that it cannot be known in essence or by definition, except in a certain respect, by being divided into these divisions or by resembling a particular instance and the like. The majority said it cannot be defined because knowledge of it, i.e., its conception, is necessary (daruri), and it cannot be defined by a real definition, and all definitions are verbal. This is the chosen position according to the author and others, for everyone knows by necessity that it exists, and it is a specific report, and the absolute report is the entire essence of this report, so from its self-evidence follows the self-evidence of the absolute. It may be answered that the obtaining of something by itself, as in presential knowledge (al-ilm al-huduri) for which mere presence suffices, is different from its conception as in acquired knowledge (al-ilm al-husuli) for which mere presence does not suffice. So the necessity of attributing existence to it does not necessitate the necessity of conceiving the attribution, for it is possible that the first is of the presential type, so the second which is of the acquired type is not necessitated. And the dispute concerns the attribution in terms of the connection of conception. From presential knowledge of the particular, acquired knowledge of its essential properties does not follow, and this is very clear. For if it were so, then the reality of generosity and bravery and all other psychological attributes would be known to us in their essence, which is clearly false. So it is said in the commentary on al-Mukhtasar. I say: It has passed that the specific report is a narration (hikaya) about the realization of its content. And the narration is only by the form of the narrated object matching this form: thus it is truthful or false. So just as the form of the subject and predicate are narrating about them, similarly the mental attribution is narrating about the real attribution, which is different in essence in existence, revealing its connection between the subject and predicate in the actual reality. Whether the intended realization is different from the narration, whether it is realized in reality or not. So it does not contradict what he chose in al-Sullam that its concept is the concept of absolute affirmation, or the intent is to explain the state of the reliable reports used in dialogues. For this reason, because the form of the subject and predicate and the attribution are narrating, they said: In a proposition, three conceptions are necessary: conception of the subject, the predicate, and the complete attribution. As for the restrictively attributed form that later scholars considered, sound intuition rules against it. And since it is established that narration is only the narrating form, knowledge of the report is only by conception and obtaining the form, so the objection is refuted. Then he returned to presentation of the proof in clarification and said: So this specific report is obtained in itself in its essence, not in its aspect, by acquired knowledge which is conception. So this specific report is conceived in its essence by self-evidence, so the absolute is likewise in its essence by self-evidence, because the essential is necessarily fixed in the rank of the essence. So its obtaining is the obtaining of the essential. He indicated during the presentation the refutation of the well-known objection that it is only complete if the essentiality of the universal and the conception of the particular in essence are established, both of which are denied, so reflect. Then this is insufficient, for knowledge of the particular does not necessitate knowledge of the absolute. How, it is possible that the particular is mentally distinct without the distinction of the absolute essential. This is very clear according to the investigators who deny the obtaining of the form, for it is possible that the particular 'bravery' is different from the absolute essential 'bravery'. So the obtaining of one does not necessitate the obtaining of the other, so the self-evidence of one does not necessitate the self-evidence of the other. Then, the knowledge related to the specific report is assertive knowledge (ilm tasdiqi), and it does not follow that from the attachment of assertion to a thing comes the attachment of conception to its essentials. Perhaps this is what the commentator of al-Mukhtasar intended, for the obtaining by itself as in knowledge of the specific report and assertion of it does not necessitate the conception of the absolute attribution which is the entirety of its essence. As for building the discussion on acquired and presential, it is a kindness to one who does not accept, for the commentator of al-Mukhtasar is among those who do not hold the unity of knower and known. And even if it is conceded that perception is by obtaining the form, there arises the objection against him by his saying: 'Except that it yields a conception, but in summary (ijmal) within the conception of the particular, without being distinguished from other than it, because the distinction of the form is a branch of the conception of the essence in detail.' And if the essence is not...
Source pages p. 121

Commentary
This passage is a discussion on the definition and epistemological nature of 'khabar' (report). The author begins by stating that the authority of the Sunnah depends on the chain of narration for us, but not for the Companions. He then defines a specific report (khabar khas) and notes that there is disagreement on whether khabar can be defined. Some, like Imam al-Ghazali, argue that it cannot be defined in a single term; it is known through division and example. The majority position, which the author considers the chosen one, holds that khabar cannot be defined because its conception is self-evident (daruri). The author then engages with an objection based on the distinction between presential (huduri) and acquired (husuli) knowledge: the self-evidence of the particular report does not necessarily lead to self-evidence of the absolute report, as presential knowledge of a thing does not require acquired knowledge of its essence. The discussion becomes technical, exploring how a report is a narration (hikaya) that confirms or denies a connection between subject and predicate. The author argues that in a proposition (qadiyya), we need three conceptions: subject, predicate, and the complete attribution. He refutes the notion of restrictive attribution. The ultimate point is that the specific report is conceived in its essence by self-evidence, and the absolute report follows because the essential is necessarily fixed. However, a counterargument is presented: knowledge of the particular does not entail knowledge of the universal, and the specific report is subject to assertive knowledge (tasdiq), not necessarily conceptual knowledge (tasawwur) of its essence. The passage ends with a critique of those who deny the obtaining of the form.

Key Terms
خبر خاص (khabar khas): A specific report that mentions a chain and corresponds to a particular instance.
علم حصولي (ilm husuli): Acquired knowledge that requires mental representation (tasawwur) of the object.
حكاية (hikaya): A narration or report that is a form matching the narrated object, enabling truth or falsehood.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the dominant view in this passage, how is khabar (report) known?
A) Through a real definition based on genus and differentia.
B) By division and example, as al-Ghazali held.
C) By necessity (darura); its conception is self-evident.
D) Only through lengthy philosophical proof.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that khabar is not subject to real definition; its conception is self-evident (daruri) and known by necessity to every rational person.

Self-Evidence of the Concept of Khabar: An Usul Dispute
Dispute between Hanafi and Razi on self-evidence of khabar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school denies the self-evidence of the concept of khabar and evaluate the refutation of Imam al-Razi's argument.

Core Idea
The concept of khabar is not self-evident; differentiation does not require full essential conception.

This passage examines a foundational epistemological question in usul al-fiqh: Is the concept of a report (khabar) self-evident? The opponent, following Imam al-Razi, argues yes, because we can distinguish reports from other speech. The Hanafi commentator refutes this, showing that differentiation only requires partial knowledge, not a full essential definition. This dispute has practical implications for whether khabar needs a formal definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opposing claim
Imam al-Razi argues that differentiating khabar from other things necessitates its conception, therefore the concept of khabar must be self-evident.
The opponent's proof is based on the necessity of conception for discrimination.
2
Examine the Hanafi refutation
The commentator denies that differentiation requires a full concept; mere presence (hudur) of particular reports or a partial distinguishing notion suffices.
The Hanafi response distinguishes between knowledge by presence and acquired conceptual knowledge.
3
Distinguish between knowledge types
The Hanafi relies on the epistemological distinction: knowledge by presence (huduri) does not require explicit conception, only acquired knowledge (husuli) does.
The opponent's argument conflates these two types.
4
Assess the proof's overreach
The opponent's argument would prove self-evidence for all concepts and imperatives (commands, prohibitions), which is not intended and shows the proof is flawed.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.
5
Conclude the dispute
The Hanafi position stands: the concept of khabar is acquired, not self-evident. Self-evidence is an intuitive matter that cannot be proven by rational argument.
The commentator refers to al-Tahrir and glosses for further discussion.

Source Text

الأصل الثاني/ السنة) ضرورة) وهي صورة الذاتي متميزة عن صورة الذات لم يكن هو منكشفاً ولا معلوماً إلا بعلم الذات، وهو وجه من وجوهه، فلم يلزم انكشاف الكنه، وفيه النزاع (فتأمل فيه، وقد يقرر الدليل بأن حصة الخبر أعني الخبر المقيد بكونه بين هذه الأطراف متصورة بكنهه وتصور المطلق لازم من تصور المقيد، فإن تصور المقيد لا يكون إلا بصورة تفصيلية صورة المطلق والقيد، فلزم تصور المطلق، وعلى هذا لا يرد عليه شيء مما ذكر، لكن يرد عليه منع كون المقيد متصوراً بالكنه، كيف وهذا في قوة أصل المدعي (وقد استدل على بداهة تصور الخبر (بالتفرقة بينه وبين غيره لا تكون إلا بالعلم بهما تصورهما (والجواب) لا نسلم (أن التفرقة) تقتضي تصور الخبر بل (لا تقتضي التصور) أصلاً بل يكفي) في التميز (الحصول) أي حصول مطلق الخبر، بل يكفي حصول الأخبار الخاصة، فإن الخصم قائل بالعلم بالقسمة والمثال وهو يكفي للتميز، ويمكن أن يقرر بما مر أنه إنما يلزم التميز مطلق العلم، وأما العلم الحصولي الذي هو التصور فكلا، وحينئذ يدفع بما مر وبأن المراد أن التفرقة بين المطلق الخبر وغيره ضرورية، فلا بد من العلم بمطلق الخبر والعلم بالمطلق لا يكون حضورياً (و) لو سلم تصور مطلق الخبر فأيضاً (لا) يقتضي (الكنه) أي تصوره بل يكفي التصور بوجه (ما) والنزاع إنما وقع في الكنه، وما في «التحرير» أن لكل من الخبر وغيره لوازم وإعطاء لوازم كل بما هو لوازم له ووضعها في موضعها لا يكون إلا بتصور الملزومات نعم لا يلزم التصور ومن حيث إنه مسمى الخبر ساقط ، لأنه يكفي لإثبات اللوازم التصور بوجه يتميز به الملزوم وأما تصور كنهه فكلا، وهذا ظاهر جداً ثم إن الدليل منقوض بسائر المفهومات فإن التفرقة بينها وبين نقائضها ضرورية، فلا بد من تصورها ضرورة، وكذا منقوض بالإنشاآت كالأوامر والنواهي، فإنه كما ينتج بداهة الخبر ينتج بداهة الإنشاء أيضاً إلا أن يقال : هذا الدليل منسوب إلى الإمام الرازي، وهو يرى بداهة جميع التصورات فتأمل فيه .، ثم اعلم أنه قيل إن الحكم بالبداهة لا يمكن أن يكتسب بالدليل، بل يكفي فيه الالتفات إلى المدرك هل حصل من غير نظر أم لا؟ فهو من الوجدانيات وبه ارتضى صاحب «التحرير» أيضاً، وقد استوفينا الكلام في حواشينا على الحاشية الزاهدية المتعلقة بشرح المواقف»
The second source: Sunnah. Necessity: The form of the essence distinguished from the form of the essence itself is not disclosed nor known except by the knowledge of the essence, and it is one of its aspects, so disclosure of the essence is not necessitated, and the dispute lies therein. Contemplate this. The proof is sometimes presented: a portion of a report, i.e., the report qualified by being between these limits, is conceived in its essence, and the conception of the absolute is necessary from the conception of the qualified, for the conception of the qualified is only by a detailed form of the absolute and the qualification, so the conception of the absolute is necessitated. According to this, nothing aforementioned is refuted, but it is refuted by the denial that the qualified is conceived in its essence—how is this? This is in the strength of the original claim. And it has been argued for the self-evidence of the conception of a report by the fact that differentiation between it and other things cannot be except by knowledge of them and their conception. The answer: we do not accept that differentiation necessitates the conception of a report; rather, it does not necessitate conception at all; rather, for discrimination, mere presence (i.e., presence of the absolute report) is sufficient; indeed, the presence of particular reports is sufficient, for the opponent holds knowledge by division and example, which suffices for discrimination. It can be argued as previously mentioned that discrimination only necessitates absolute knowledge, but as for acquired knowledge which is conception, no. Then it is repelled by what has passed and by saying the intended meaning is that the differentiation between the absolute report and other things is necessary, so knowledge of the absolute report is necessary, and knowledge of the absolute is not present. And even if the conception of the absolute report is conceded, it still does not necessitate the essence; rather, a partial conception is sufficient. The dispute lies only in the essence. What is in al-Tahrir: every report and other things have necessary concomitants, and assigning the necessary concomitants of each as its necessary concomitants and placing them in their places cannot be except by the conception of the subject matters. Indeed, the conception from the standpoint that it is named 'report' is invalid, because it suffices for proving the necessary concomitants to have a conception that distinguishes the subject matter, but as for the conception of its essence, no. This is very evident. Then the proof is refuted by other concepts, for the differentiation between them and their opposites is necessary, so their conception is necessary. Likewise, it is refuted by imperatives like commands and prohibitions, for just as it produces the self-evidence of a report, it also produces the self-evidence of imperative words, unless it is said: this proof is attributed to Imam al-Razi, and he holds all concepts to be self-evident; so contemplate this. Then know that it is said that the ruling of self-evidence cannot be acquired through proof; rather, it suffices to examine the perceiver: is it obtained without reflection or not? It is among the intuitive matters, and the author of al-Tahrir also approved this. We have fully discussed this in our glosses on the Zaghdiyya gloss related to the commentary on al-Mawaqif.
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage is a dispute concerning the self-evidence (badahah) of the concept of khabar (report). The text begins by stating the second source of Islamic law: the Sunnah. It then delves into an epistemological argument about whether the concept of a report is self-evident or acquired. The opponent, likely following Imam al-Razi, argues that the concept of khabar is self-evident because differentiating it from other things requires its conception. The Hanafi commentator refutes this by maintaining that differentiation does not necessitate a detailed conception; a mere presentational awareness (hudur) or particular instances suffice. The distinction between knowledge by presence (huduri) and acquired knowledge (husuli) is crucial: the former does not require a fully explicit concept. The commentator also argues that even if we concede some conception, it does not reach the essence (kunn) of khabar—only a partial distinguishing notion is needed. The argument is further weakened because it would apply equally to other concepts and imperatives, contradicting the specificity of khabar. Finally, the commentator notes that judgments of self-evidence cannot be established by proof; they are intuitive. Thus, the Hanafi position is that the concept of khabar is not self-evident in the strong sense required by the opponent.

Key Terms
خبر (khabar): A report or statement that can be true or false; a linguistic expression conveying information.
بداهة (badahah): Self-evidence; knowledge that is immediate and does not require proof or reflection.
كنه (kunn): The true essence or quiddity of a thing; deep conceptual understanding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, what is the status of the concept of khabar?
A) It is self-evident and requires no proof
B) It is not self-evident; it is an acquired concept
C) It is self-evident only for commands
D) It is impossible to conceive

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi position holds that the concept of khabar is not self-evident; it is an acquired concept requiring definition, and self-evidence cannot be proven rationally.

Truth is Not Conformity to Belief
Defining truth and falsehood with consensus example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi definition of sidq as conformity to reality (waqi') and derive this from the consensus example of the Jew.

Core Idea
Truth is objective correspondence to reality, not to the speaker's belief.

This passage opens by clarifying the meaning of truth and falsehood in the context of reports. Using a vivid consensus example of a Jew testifying to Islam, the author argues that truth is not defined by alignment with the speaker's belief but by conformity to external reality. This lesson focuses on that definition and the proof from consensus.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Locate the terms sidq and kadhb in the Arabic passage. Note how the author connects them to intention (qasd).
The author says lying is attributed to intention, so negating a lie negates the intention, not the act itself.
2
Examine the consensus proof
Find the phrase 'al-ijma' 'ala anna al-yahudi...' and understand the scenario: a Jew says 'Islam is true.' The ruling is that we judge him truthful despite his false belief.
This consensus is cited as decisive (qati).
3
Understand the implication
If truth were conformity to belief, the Jew's statement would be false (since it contradicts his false belief). But the consensus judges it truthful, so truth cannot be conformity to belief.
Thus truth is conformity to reality (waqi').
4
Formulate the Hanafi definition
State: al-sidq is the correspondence of a report to reality, independent of the reporter's belief. Falsehood is the absence of that correspondence.
This definition underpins the classification of reports in the rest of the passage.

The second source / Sunnah. (It is said that) when (actions) are attributed to people of intention, it is immediately understood that they are done with intention, so that is the meaning. Even if intention is not part of the concept of those actions, lying is among these actions; thus it is immediately understood that it is done with intention, so what is negated is the intention, not absolute lying. And (the commentator) said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: What settles the dispute is the consensus that if a Jew says 'Islam is true', we judge him truthful, even though it contradicts his false belief; and if he says the opposite, we judge him lying, even though it agrees with his false belief. So it is known that conformity with belief is not included in the concept of truth, nor is it its essence, so understand. Also: The report is either known to be true necessarily by itself – that is, by virtue of being a report emanating from reporters – and that is mutawatir; or known to be true necessarily by something else – and that is the report that agrees with necessary knowledge that is known whether reported or not, such as 'one is half of two', for its content is known necessarily whether reported or not; or known to be true by reflection, such as the report of Allah the Exalted and the Messenger, for its truth is known through the intermediary that the report emanates from Allah and His Messenger, and everything that emanates from them is truthful. This is for one who has not reached the station of witnessing and intuition; as for one who has reached these two stations, it is known to be true necessarily. And the report of the people of consensus, the report that agrees with correct reflection in certain matters such as geometry and arithmetic. On this basis, the solitary report accompanied by contextual indicators is excluded, so an objection arises regarding the incompleteness of the classification for those who consider it knowledge-yielding, unless it is included in what yields knowledge by reflection or is known to be false necessarily or by reflection. And it is every report that contradicts – that is, contradicts what is known to be true – or nothing is known about it, i.e., about its truth or falsity. So one of them may be presumed, like the report of a solitary just narrator, for it is presumed truthful with the possibility of forgetfulness and error; and the report of a solitary liar, for it is presumed false with the possibility of truth, for a liar may sometimes be truthful; and sometimes they are equal in truth and falsehood, like the unknown narrator, i.e., the solitary report of one whose justice and lying are unknown. So they said, but there is an objection against it: his report is considered a proof according to some; indeed his testimony is accepted by the Imam al-Humam (may Allah be pleased with him); indeed his narration is also accepted according to what is transmitted from him outside the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah). So how can his report be equal in truth and falsehood? Rather, it is presumed truthful, but the presumption is weak. Some Zahiris said: Everything whose truth is not known, its falsehood is known. But what appears is that they meant by it decisive conformity (like the report of a claimant of prophethood), i.e., they analogized to the report of a claimant of prophethood, for when his truth is not known by showing miracles, he is decisively judged false, because reports are equal. And this (the Zahiri statement) is false because it necessitates the raising of two contradictories, which necessitates their conjunction. Since the falsehood of each necessitates the truth of the other, their falsehood necessitates their truth. There is no impossibility in the relationship between an impossibility and its opposite; rather it is the truth as established by the consequent syllogisms. For detail, there is another place concerning the reports of two unknowns with two contradictories. Its detail: If an unknown person reports a report, his truth is not known, so his falsehood is known. Then if another unknown reports its opposite, his truth is not known, so his falsehood is also known, thus necessitating the raising of the two contradictories. That itself being impossible necessitates their conjunction. The commentator of al-Mukhtasar confined himself to the necessity of their conjunction, and building on this approach lengthens the distance, for their raising is impossible like their conjunction. So it is better to formulate his statement: If an unknown reports his report, his truth is not known, so his falsehood is known, then the truth of its opposite is known. Similarly in reporting its opposite. But the objector may object that if the falsehood of the unknown's report is known, then the truth of its opposite is known; so the report of that opposite by another unknown is not something whose truth is not known; rather it is known because its content agrees with knowledge, whether necessary or theoretical. But the matter is easy, for the invalidity of this opinion is necessary and independent of explanation, and what is mentioned is a reminder, so understand. I say: This, i.e., the necessity of raising the two contradictories necessitating their conjunction, is based on the fact that conformity to reality is considered in knowledge as is understood customarily, linguistically, and legally. Because if it also includes ignorance, then raising the two contradictories is not necessary in reality. In that case, the mentioned classification into known truth, known falsehood, presumed one of them, and equal is not exhaustive, since a report that agrees with compound ignorance – which is decisive judgment not conforming to reality – contains neither knowledge (due to lack of conformity) nor presumption nor doubt, because in them there is allowance for the opposing side, whether preponderant or equal, whereas the person of compound ignorance is decisive with no allowance at all for the opposite. So reflect, for the matter is easy: The purpose of the classification is not exhaustiveness, but rather to refute those who claim limitation to known truth and known falsehood. So reflect. This. Then he answered their analogy by saying: As for disbelieving a claimant of prophethood when his truth does not appear, it is contrary to custom, i.e., disbelieving him because this claim without evidence is contrary to custom, and custom necessitates knowledge decisively. So by this aspect his falsehood is known, not because his truth is not known. So your analogy is flawed. And it is said: Custom necessitates knowledge as presumption, so he is presumed false, so there is no knowledge originally to analogize from. But this contradicts necessity, for a claimant of prophethood unable to perform a miracle is decisively false without doubt, so understand. And also: Another classification of the report is: Mutawatir if it is a report of a group that yields knowledge by itself, i.e., by virtue of being a report of these reporters, not by separate contextual indicators, unlike necessary indicators from the circumstances of the speaker, the listener, and the subject matter, for these circumstances have a role in yielding knowledge, as is not hidden. Therefore, due to its being knowledge-yielding with respect to the circumstances of the speaker, listener, and content, the number of tawatur varies according to the variation of the report. For reports from fewer just persons may yield knowledge while those from more wicked persons do not. Similarly, with variation of listeners: reports in the presence of a ruler who punishes liars from fewer persons yield knowledge while reports from more persons elsewhere do not. Similarly with variation of content: reports of the king's confidants about his secrets, even if they are fewer, yield knowledge while reports of more others do not. All this is necessary. And if a group report is not like that, then it is the report of one. This classification encompasses all reports, but apparently the intention is to classify the report narrated from the Messenger of Allah and his Companions, for that is the goal of the usuli's consideration, and what follows supports it. If a single man narrates it, i.e., there is only one narrator in one level of the chain, even if there are more narrators in another level, then it is gharib. If two narrate it – and this does not refer to two narrators in every level but rather there are two and no more in one level, even if there are more in another – then it is aziz. And it is not a condition for the sahih report; rather gharib is also sahih and obligatory to act upon because there is no difference in the proofs indicating authority. And it is not a condition for al-Bukhari, Imam Muhammad ibn Isma'il, author of al-Sahih, may Allah's mercy be upon him, in the correct opinion, even if some claim it is a condition for aziz.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Muslim al-Thubut, a commentary on the usul al-fiqh matn Musallam al-Thubut, discusses the second source of Islamic law: the Sunnah, but focuses on the epistemological classification of reports (akhbar). The author begins by clarifying the concept of truth (sidq) and falsehood (kadhb). He argues that when actions like lying are attributed to deliberate agents, they are immediately understood as intentional; thus, negating a lie negates the intention, not the mere utterance. He supports this with consensus (ijma'): if a Jew says 'Islam is true,' we consider him truthful despite his false belief, proving that truth is not conformity to belief. The passage then classifies reports by how their truth is known: (1) necessarily by itself (mutawatir), (2) necessarily by something else (agreement with necessary knowledge like mathematical truths), (3) by reflection (reports of Allah and the Messenger for those not at the level of witnessing, and reports of ahl al-ijma' on certainties). Solitary reports with contextual indicators may yield knowledge by reflection. Reports can be presumed truthful (from a just narrator), presumed false (from a known liar), or balanced between truth and falsehood (from an unknown narrator), though the commentator notes that an unknown narrator's report is still accepted by some, so it is presumed truthful weakly. The author then refutes the Zahiri claim that whatever is not known to be true is known to be false, showing it leads to absurdities like the raising of contradictories. Finally, he presents another classification: mutawatir (group report yielding knowledge by itself) and ahad (solitary). The number for tawatur varies with circumstances of speaker, listener, and content. Within ahad, he defines gharib (single narrator at one level) and aziz (two narrators at one level), stating both can be sahih and obligatory to act upon, contrary to some who require aziz for al-Bukhari's standard. The commentator emphasizes that the main concern is reports from the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). This passage demonstrates the Hanafi usuli approach to classifying reports based on epistemic certainty, using rational analysis and consensus as tools.

Key Terms
الصدق (al-sidq): Conformity of the report to reality (waqi'), not to the belief of the reporter
الكذب (al-kadhb): Non-conformity of the report to reality, regardless of the reporter's belief

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a Jew who says 'Islam is true' considered truthful according to the passage?
A) Because his statement agrees with his belief.
B) Because truth is conformity to reality, not belief.
C) Because his statement is a lie.
D) Because consensus requires it.

Key Takeaway
Truth (sidq) is objective conformity to reality, as proven by consensus; it is not defined by the reporter's belief.

Al-Sunnah as Source
intermediate
12 min
Epistemic Classification of Reports
Classifying reports by epistemic certainty

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify a given report into one of the five epistemic categories: known truth (mutawatir or by necessity/reflection), presumed truth, presumed false, equal, or compound ignorance.

Core Idea
Reports are not all equal; their truth is known necessarily, by reflection, by presumption, or not at all.

After establishing the definition of truth, the passage provides a detailed classification of reports based on how their truthfulness is known. This lesson maps these categories: reports known to be true necessarily (mutawatir or by agreement with necessary knowledge), known by reflection (reports from Allah, the Prophet, and ahl al-ijma on certainties), reports presumed true (from a just narrator), presumed false (from a liar), and reports with equal probability (unknown narrator). It also acknowledges a category of compound ignorance where a person is decisively wrong without awareness.

Guided Path
1
List the epistemic categories
From the passage, enumerate the types of reports: (1) known truth necessarily by itself, (2) known truth necessarily by something else, (3) known truth by reflection, (4) presumed truthful, (5) presumed false, (6) equal probability, and (7) compound ignorance.
The author notes the classification is not exhaustive but sufficient to refute those who claim only known truth or known falsehood.
2
Define mutawatir and its conditions
Identify the definition of mutawatir: a report from a group that yields knowledge by itself, not by external indicators. Note that the number for tawatur varies by speaker, listener, and content.
Hanafis do not fix a minimum number.
3
Distinguish knowledge by reflection from presumption
Knowledge by reflection (nazar) is certain but requires reasoning (e.g., reports from Allah, Prophet). Presumption (zann) is probable but not certain (e.g., just narrator).
The report of a just narrator is presumed true but may be erroneous.
4
Identify the categories for ahad reports
Ahad reports are those not meeting tawatur conditions. They can be gharib (single at one level) or aziz (two at one level). Both are valid proofs if reliable.
Gharib can be sahih and binding.
5
Recognize the role of contextual indicators (qara'in)
Some ahad reports with supporting qara'in may yield knowledge by reflection, thus falling into the third category.
This solves the objection about classification completeness.

ثم أجاب عن قياسهم بقوله : (وأما تكذيب مدعي الرسالة عند عدم ظهور صدقه (فإنه بخلاف العادة) أي تكذيبه بسبب كون هذه الدعوى من غير بينة خلاف العادة (وهي) أي العادة توجب العلم قطعاً) فبهذا الوجه يعلم كذبه، لا لأنه لم يعلم صدقه، فقياسكم مع الفارق (وقيل) توجب العادة العلم (ظناً) فيظن بكذبه ، فلا علم في الأصل حتى يقاس عليه، لكن هذا خلاف الضرورة، فإن مدعي الرسالة العاجز عن إقامة معجزة مقطوع الكذب من غير ريب فافهم (وأيضاً) تقسيم آخر للخبر هو (متواتر إن كان خبر جماعة يفيد العلم بنفسه) أي من حيث إنه خبر هؤلاء المخبرين (لا) بالقرائن المنفصلة عن الخبر (بخلاف القرائن (اللازمة) له (من أحوال في المخبر) المتكلم (والمخبر ) السامع (والمخبر عنه) وهو مضمون الخبر فإن لهذه الأحوال دخلاً في إفادة العلم كما لا يخفى (ولذلك) أي لأجل كونه مفيداً للعلم بالنظر إلى أحوال المتكلم والسامع ومضمون الخبر يتفاوت عدد التواتر) بتفاوت الخبر، فأخبار العدول الأقلين ربما يفيد العلم دون الفسقة الأكثرين وكذا بتفاوت ،السامعين، فإن الأخبار عند ذي سلطنة يؤذي من كذب عنده من أقلين يفيد العلم دون أكثرين عند غيره، وكذا بتفاوت مضمون الخبر، فإن أخبار دخاليل الملك عن أسراره وإن كانوا أقلين يفيد العلم دون أكثرين غيرهم، وهذا كله ضروري (وإلا يكن خبر جماعة كذلك فخبر (الواحد وهذا التقسيم يشمل جميع الأخبار، لكن الظاهر أن المقصود تقسيم الخبر المروي عن رسول الله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم، إذ هو المطمح لنظر الأصولي ويؤيده ما بعده (فإن رواه) أي خبر الواحد رجل (واحد) فقط بأن يكون في السند واحد في مرتبة وإن كان الرواة أكثر من مرتبة أخرى فهو الغريب وإن رواه (اثنان ولا ينقض عن اثنين الرواة في كل مرتبة ولا يزيد عليه في مرتبة ما وإن زاد في أخرى فهو العزيز وليس شرطاً للصحيح من الخبر فالغريب أيضاً يكون صحيحاً و واجب العمل لعدم فرق الأدلة الدالة على الحجية (ولا) (للبخاري) الإمام محمد بن إسماعيل صاحب الصحيح رحمة الله عليه (في) المذهب (الصحيح) وإن زعم البعض أنه شرط العزة
The second source / Sunnah. (It is said that) when (actions) are attributed to people of intention, it is immediately understood that they are done with intention, so that is the meaning. Even if intention is not part of the concept of those actions, lying is among these actions; thus it is immediately understood that it is done with intention, so what is negated is the intention, not absolute lying. And (the commentator) said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: What settles the dispute is the consensus that if a Jew says 'Islam is true', we judge him truthful, even though it contradicts his false belief; and if he says the opposite, we judge him lying, even though it agrees with his false belief. So it is known that conformity with belief is not included in the concept of truth, nor is it its essence, so understand. Also: The report is either known to be true necessarily by itself – that is, by virtue of being a report emanating from reporters – and that is mutawatir; or known to be true necessarily by something else – and that is the report that agrees with necessary knowledge that is known whether reported or not, such as 'one is half of two', for its content is known necessarily whether reported or not; or known to be true by reflection, such as the report of Allah the Exalted and the Messenger, for its truth is known through the intermediary that the report emanates from Allah and His Messenger, and everything that emanates from them is truthful. This is for one who has not reached the station of witnessing and intuition; as for one who has reached these two stations, it is known to be true necessarily. And the report of the people of consensus, the report that agrees with correct reflection in certain matters such as geometry and arithmetic. On this basis, the solitary report accompanied by contextual indicators is excluded, so an objection arises regarding the incompleteness of the classification for those who consider it knowledge-yielding, unless it is included in what yields knowledge by reflection or is known to be false necessarily or by reflection. And it is every report that contradicts – that is, contradicts what is known to be true – or nothing is known about it, i.e., about its truth or falsity. So one of them may be presumed, like the report of a solitary just narrator, for it is presumed truthful with the possibility of forgetfulness and error; and the report of a solitary liar, for it is presumed false with the possibility of truth, for a liar may sometimes be truthful; and sometimes they are equal in truth and falsehood, like the unknown narrator, i.e., the solitary report of one whose justice and lying are unknown. So they said, but there is an objection against it: his report is considered a proof according to some; indeed his testimony is accepted by the Imam al-Humam (may Allah be pleased with him); indeed his narration is also accepted according to what is transmitted from him outside the apparent narration (Zahir al-Riwayah). So how can his report be equal in truth and falsehood? Rather, it is presumed truthful, but the presumption is weak. Some Zahiris said: Everything whose truth is not known, its falsehood is known. But what appears is that they meant by it decisive conformity (like the report of a claimant of prophethood), i.e., they analogized to the report of a claimant of prophethood, for when his truth is not known by showing miracles, he is decisively judged false, because reports are equal. And this (the Zahiri statement) is false because it necessitates the raising of two contradictories, which necessitates their conjunction. Since the falsehood of each necessitates the truth of the other, their falsehood necessitates their truth. There is no impossibility in the relationship between an impossibility and its opposite; rather it is the truth as established by the consequent syllogisms. For detail, there is another place concerning the reports of two unknowns with two contradictories. Its detail: If an unknown person reports a report, his truth is not known, so his falsehood is known. Then if another unknown reports its opposite, his truth is not known, so his falsehood is also known, thus necessitating the raising of the two contradictories. That itself being impossible necessitates their conjunction. The commentator of al-Mukhtasar confined himself to the necessity of their conjunction, and building on this approach lengthens the distance, for their raising is impossible like their conjunction. So it is better to formulate his statement: If an unknown reports his report, his truth is not known, so his falsehood is known, then the truth of its opposite is known. Similarly in reporting its opposite. But the objector may object that if the falsehood of the unknown's report is known, then the truth of its opposite is known; so the report of that opposite by another unknown is not something whose truth is not known; rather it is known because its content agrees with knowledge, whether necessary or theoretical. But the matter is easy, for the invalidity of this opinion is necessary and independent of explanation, and what is mentioned is a reminder, so understand. I say: This, i.e., the necessity of raising the two contradictories necessitating their conjunction, is based on the fact that conformity to reality is considered in knowledge as is understood customarily, linguistically, and legally. Because if it also includes ignorance, then raising the two contradictories is not necessary in reality. In that case, the mentioned classification into known truth, known falsehood, presumed one of them, and equal is not exhaustive, since a report that agrees with compound ignorance – which is decisive judgment not conforming to reality – contains neither knowledge (due to lack of conformity) nor presumption nor doubt, because in them there is allowance for the opposing side, whether preponderant or equal, whereas the person of compound ignorance is decisive with no allowance at all for the opposite. So reflect, for the matter is easy: The purpose of the classification is not exhaustiveness, but rather to refute those who claim limitation to known truth and known falsehood. So reflect. This. Then he answered their analogy by saying: As for disbelieving a claimant of prophethood when his truth does not appear, it is contrary to custom, i.e., disbelieving him because this claim without evidence is contrary to custom, and custom necessitates knowledge decisively. So by this aspect his falsehood is known, not because his truth is not known. So your analogy is flawed. And it is said: Custom necessitates knowledge as presumption, so he is presumed false, so there is no knowledge originally to analogize from. But this contradicts necessity, for a claimant of prophethood unable to perform a miracle is decisively false without doubt, so understand. And also: Another classification of the report is: Mutawatir if it is a report of a group that yields knowledge by itself, i.e., by virtue of being a report of these reporters, not by separate contextual indicators, unlike necessary indicators from the circumstances of the speaker, the listener, and the subject matter, for these circumstances have a role in yielding knowledge, as is not hidden. Therefore, due to its being knowledge-yielding with respect to the circumstances of the speaker, listener, and content, the number of tawatur varies according to the variation of the report. For reports from fewer just persons may yield knowledge while those from more wicked persons do not. Similarly, with variation of listeners: reports in the presence of a ruler who punishes liars from fewer persons yield knowledge while reports from more persons elsewhere do not. Similarly with variation of content: reports of the king's confidants about his secrets, even if they are fewer, yield knowledge while reports of more others do not. All this is necessary. And if a group report is not like that, then it is the report of one. This classification encompasses all reports, but apparently the intention is to classify the report narrated from the Messenger of Allah and his Companions, for that is the goal of the usuli's consideration, and what follows supports it. If a single man narrates it, i.e., there is only one narrator in one level of the chain, even if there are more narrators in another level, then it is gharib. If two narrate it – and this does not refer to two narrators in every level but rather there are two and no more in one level, even if there are more in another – then it is aziz. And it is not a condition for the sahih report; rather gharib is also sahih and obligatory to act upon because there is no difference in the proofs indicating authority. And it is not a condition for al-Bukhari, Imam Muhammad ibn Isma'il, author of al-Sahih, may Allah's mercy be upon him, in the correct opinion, even if some claim it is a condition for aziz.
Source pages p. 133

Commentary
This passage from Sharh Muslim al-Thubut, a commentary on the usul al-fiqh matn Musallam al-Thubut, discusses the second source of Islamic law: the Sunnah, but focuses on the epistemological classification of reports (akhbar). The author begins by clarifying the concept of truth (sidq) and falsehood (kadhb). He argues that when actions like lying are attributed to deliberate agents, they are immediately understood as intentional; thus, negating a lie negates the intention, not the mere utterance. He supports this with consensus (ijma'): if a Jew says 'Islam is true,' we consider him truthful despite his false belief, proving that truth is not conformity to belief. The passage then classifies reports by how their truth is known: (1) necessarily by itself (mutawatir), (2) necessarily by something else (agreement with necessary knowledge like mathematical truths), (3) by reflection (reports of Allah and the Messenger for those not at the level of witnessing, and reports of ahl al-ijma' on certainties). Solitary reports with contextual indicators may yield knowledge by reflection. Reports can be presumed truthful (from a just narrator), presumed false (from a known liar), or balanced between truth and falsehood (from an unknown narrator), though the commentator notes that an unknown narrator's report is still accepted by some, so it is presumed truthful weakly. The author then refutes the Zahiri claim that whatever is not known to be true is known to be false, showing it leads to absurdities like the raising of contradictories. Finally, he presents another classification: mutawatir (group report yielding knowledge by itself) and ahad (solitary). The number for tawatur varies with circumstances of speaker, listener, and content. Within ahad, he defines gharib (single narrator at one level) and aziz (two narrators at one level), stating both can be sahih and obligatory to act upon, contrary to some who require aziz for al-Bukhari's standard. The commentator emphasizes that the main concern is reports from the Prophet Muhammad (peace be upon him). This passage demonstrates the Hanafi usuli approach to classifying reports based on epistemic certainty, using rational analysis and consensus as tools.

Key Terms
المتواتر (al-mutawatir): A report from a group that yields necessary knowledge by itself, without separate contextual indicators
خبر الواحد (khabar al-wahid): A report that is not mutawatir; it may be gharib (single narrator at one level) or aziz (two narrators at one level)
الغريب (al-gharib): A hadith with a single narrator at one level of the chain
العزيز (al-aziz): A hadith with two narrators at one level

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Known truth by reflection
B) Presumed false
C) Equal probability
D) Known false by necessity

Key Takeaway
Reports are classified by epistemic certainty into categories ranging from necessary knowledge to weak presumption; the purpose is to refute the claim that only knowledge or falsehood exists.

Refuting the Zahiri Claim: Logical and Custom-Based Arguments
Refuting the Zahiri position on unknown reports

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply both the logical argument (contradiction) and the custom-based argument (ada) to refute the Zahiri position that unknown truth equals known falsehood.

Core Idea
Not knowing a report is true does not make it known false; otherwise, contradictory reports would both be false, an absurdity.

A group of Zahiris claimed that any report whose truth is not known is known to be false, analogizing to a claimant of prophethood who fails to produce miracles. The Hanafi rebuttal uses two arguments: a logical reductio showing that this leads to contradictory conclusions, and a custom-based argument that the claimant's falsehood is known by custom (ada), not by the mere lack of known truth. This lesson unpacks both arguments.

Guided Path
1
State the Zahiri claim and their proof
The Zahiris say: 'Everything whose truth is not known, its falsehood is known.' They analogize to a claimant of prophethood who does not show miracles – his falsehood is known.
They treat all reports on a par (al-ikhbar sawasiyya).
2
Follow the logical reductio ad absurdum
Consider two unknown narrators giving contradictory reports (A and not-A). If both are unknown as to truth, then according to the Zahiri, both are known to be false. That would mean both their opposites (not-A and A) are true, leading to a contradiction (A and not-A both true). This is impossible.
The argument shows the Zahiri position leads to the conjunction of two contradictories.
3
Understand the objector's nuance
An objector might say that if the falsehood of the first report is known, then the truth of its opposite is known, so the second report is not 'unknown' – it is known to be true. This breaks the assumption.
The author dismisses this as a minor nuance; the unreasonableness of the Zahiri view is clear anyway.
4
Analyze the custom-based rebuttal
The claimant of prophethood is not known false because his truth is unknown, but because his claim without evidence is contrary to established custom (ada). Custom yields decisive knowledge in that case.
Thus the analogy is flawed (farq).
